The NeckerâZeno Model for Bistable Perception
Harald Atmanspacher, Thomas Filk
First published: 11 September 2013
https://doi.org/10.1111/tops.12044
Citations: 15
About
Sections
 

Abstract
A novel conceptual framework for theoretical psychology is presented and illustrated for the example of bistable perception. A basic formal feature of this framework is the non-commutativity of operations acting on mental states. A corresponding model for the bistable perception of ambiguous stimuli, the NeckerâZeno model, is sketched and some empirical evidence for it so far is described. It is discussed how a temporal non-locality of mental states, predicted by the model, can be understood and tested.

1. Introduction
This contribution presents a novel perspective on a class of long known phenomena in psychology and psychophysics: the bistable perception of ambiguous stimuli. Our approach leads to empirical predictions and observations beyond those of previous work, but we see its chief significance in a conceptual and formal framework differing from much of traditional psychology. This framework consists of a conception of mental states and operations acting on them which is both more sophisticated and, we argue, more plausible and insightful than many traditional accounts.

1.1. Operations on states
In a general sense, any change in the state Ï of a system (physical or mental) can be described by an operation acting on this state. In physical systems, such operations are often observables, for instance, position, momentum, energy, and others. An important case for the action of an observable on a state is the measurement of an observable. Measurement results for observables A and B are typically quantified by real-valued numbers a, b,â¦

A physical standard example for this terminology is a material measurement apparatus M recording particular properties of a material system S in state Ï (with some probability urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0001). In basic physics, observables occur in canonically conjugate pairs, such as position and momentum, exhibiting particular well-defined invariance principles and conservation laws.

In psychology, we are concerned with a mental system S, usually of an individual subject being in a state Ï. In contrast to physics, there are no canonically conjugate pairs of observables. For instance, a questionnaire as a measuring device M can contain any questions one may want to choose as observables to be measured in terms of answers as measurement results.

If the interaction of M with S is weak, then it leaves no significant effect on S, whereasâconverselyâthe effect of S on M is of course the registration of the measurement result. In case of strong interactions, this registration is always accompanied by a non-negligible influence of M on the state Ï of S.

In physics, the distinction of weak and strong interactions is one major criterion for delineating classical systems from quantum systems. In addition, quantum measurementsâin contrast to classical measurementsâare not simply registrations of pre-existing facts, but they also âestablishâ the fact which is registered. The key feature of this classical-quantum distinction distinction is mathematically codified in terms of the commutativity or non-commutativity, respectively, of observables. Referring to actions of an observable on a state, A(Ï) or B(Ï), these actions either leave the state as it was, or they change it.

As a consequence, the successive action of two operations on a state can be commutative, A(B(Ï)) = B(A(Ï)), or non-commutative, A(B(Ï))â B(A(Ï)). In physics, the behavior of a system is called classical in the former case, whereas the latter case refers to quantum behavior. From this perspective, quantum theory can be viewed as a general systems theory of both commuting and non-commuting operations, and classical physics is restricted to the special case of commuting operations alone (cf. Primas, 1990).

Whenever the sequence of operations on the state Ï of a system S makes a difference for the final result, measurements are non-commutative. Many complex systems must be expected to exhibit non-commutative properties in this sense. In psychology, where virtually every interaction with a mental state changes that state uncontrollably, and where mental states are often literally established by measurements, it is highly plausible to argue that non-commutativity should be the ubiquitous rule.

There are additional important quantum concepts for mental systems besides the non-commutativity of observables. Among them are the dispersiveness of mental states, non-local correlations, violations of Bell-type inequalities, interference effects, non-Boolean logic, and alternatives to standard rules of probability theory. They are explained and related to one another in the introduction to this issue (Wang, Busemeyer, Atmanspacher, & Pothos, 2013; see also Atmanspacher & RÃ¶mer, 2012).1 / 2ChatGPTChatGPTThe article from the Wiley Online Library, "The NeckerâZeno Model for Bistable Perception" by Harald Atmanspacher and Thomas Filk, presents a novel conceptual framework for understanding bistable perception in psychology. This framework is based on the non-commutativity of operations acting on mental states, drawing parallels with quantum mechanics. This is known as bistable perception.Operations on Mental States: The authors describe mental states using a formalism similar to that used in physics. Changes in a mental state are described as operations, akin to physical observables like position or momentum.Non-Commutativity in Psychology: In contrast to classical physics, where operations (measurements) are commutative (order doesn't matter), the authors propose that in psychology, the sequence of operations on a mental state can be non-commutative. This means the order of operations (like mental tasks or stimuli) can affect the final mental state.Quantum Concepts in Mental Systems: The paper suggests that several quantum concepts, such as non-commutativity, non-local correlations, and interference effects, could be applicable to mental systems. This implies that mental states and their changes could be more complex and interconnected than traditionally thought.Implications for Psychological Measurements: In psychology, interactions with a mental state often change that state, and measurements can establish new mental states. This unpredictability and the establishment of states through measurement align with quantum mechanics principles, suggesting a quantum-like behavior of mental systems.Broader Quantum Framework: The authors argue that quantum theory can be viewed as a general systems theory that includes both commuting (classical) and non-commuting (quantum) operations. This perspective could provide a more comprehensive understanding of complex systems, including the human mind.Potential for New Insights: By applying these quantum concepts to psychology, the authors open the door for new insights into how the mind processes information and how mental states evolve.This approach represents a significant shift from traditional psychological models, proposing a more complex and interconnected view of mental processes that aligns with some principles of quantum mechanics. It suggests that the mind might operate in ways that are more akin to quantum systems than classical ones.You1.2. Non-commuting operations in psychology
Traditionally, psychology has not considered effects of non-commutative operations throughout its history. But there are quite a number of psychological phenomena showing clear features of such an approach. Some of them, which have been worked out in recent years, are decision (or judgment) processes, semantic associations, bistable perception, learning, and order effects in questionnaires. For some more details and references see the review by Atmanspacher (2011), which highlights this line of thinking in Sec. 4.7. Ideas to understand consciousness in terms of actual quantum processes in the brain, also reviewed in the same article, are at variance with the approach applied here.

It should be noted at this point that the models developed in the mentioned areas are not all explicitly based on non-commuting operations. They feature various related concepts such as incompatibility, complementarity, entanglement, partial Boolean logic, dispersive states, quantum probability, uncertainty relations, etc. However, these concepts bear close formal relationships with the property of non-commutativity and its ramifications (cf. Atmanspacher, RÃ¶mer, & Walach, 2002; Atmanspacher, Filk, & RÃ¶mer, 2006, Filk & RÃ¶mer, 2011; more technically: RÃ©dei, 1996, RÃ©dei & Summers, 2007).

Bistable perception is a widely known and intensely studied phenomenon for a long time (for reviews, see Blake & Logothetis, 2002; Long & Toppino, 2004; Kornmeier & Bach, 2012). In the present contribution, we give an overview of the NeckerâZeno model of bistable perception developed on the basis of non-commuting operations, which we tested in recent years (Atmanspacher, Filk, & RÃ¶mer, 2004; Atmanspacher, Bach, Filk, Kornmeier, & RÃ¶mer, 2008; Atmanspacher, Filk, & RÃ¶mer, 2009; Atmanspacher & Filk, 2010, 2012).1

The subsequent Section 2.1. sketches some formal basics of the NeckerâZeno model which are inspired by the quantum Zeno effect (Misra & Sudarshan, 1977), essentially predicting the deceleration of the decay of an unstable state under observation. In Section 2.2., we present a number of empirical tests of predictions of the model using the Necker cube (Necker, 1832) as an experimental paradigm with low semantic content.

Section 3. recapitulates the arguments that Bell (1966) and others introduced in the 1960s to distinguish the classical concept of local, independent elements of reality from the quantum notion of non-local correlations between entangled subsystems. This section focuses on physical quantum systems and prepares the reader to the idea of studying whether mental states can be temporally non-local.

In Section 4.1., we introduce a so-called temporal Bell inequality, first formulated by Leggett and Garg (1985) for physical systems, and we show how it may relate to mental states in bistable perception. Section 4.2. demonstrates how such a temporal Bell inequality is violated in the NeckerâZeno model. Such violations would imply temporally non-local mental states, and we address some options of how to test this empirically.ChatGPTChatGPTThe section 1.2 of the article "The NeckerâZeno Model for Bistable Perception" delves deeper into the application of non-commutative operations in psychology, a concept traditionally not considered in the field. Here's a breakdown of the key points:Non-Commutative Operations in Psychology: The article notes that while psychology has not historically focused on non-commutative operations, recent research in areas like decision-making, semantic associations, and learning shows features of this approach. The article discusses the NeckerâZeno model, developed to explain bistable perception using non-commutative operations.Quantum Zeno Effect: The NeckerâZeno model is inspired by the quantum Zeno effect, which predicts the slowing down of the decay of an unstable state under observation. This sets the stage for exploring the possibility of non-local correlations in mental states.Temporal Bell Inequality: The authors introduce the concept of a temporal Bell inequality, originally formulated for physical systems, and discuss its potential application to mental states in bistable perception.Violations of Temporal Bell Inequality: The NeckerâZeno model suggests that such violations could imply temporally non-local mental states. It suggests that mental states might exhibit properties akin to quantum systems, challenging traditional views in psychology.You2. The NeckerâZeno model
2.1. Basic formal elements
The mathematical framework of the NeckerâZeno model is inspired by the mathematics of a two-state quantum Zeno effect (Misra & Sudarshan, 1977). However, we emphasize that we do not consider a phenomenon in the sense of quantum physics to be responsible for the switching dynamics. Our formal description exclusively refers to mental states and mental observables and their dynamics. It does not even remotely address potential candidates for underlying brain activity.2

The details of the NeckerâZeno model have been described in previous publications (Atmanspacher et al., 2004, 2008, 2009; Atmanspacher & Filk, 2010, 2012). Here, we restrict ourselves to a brief review of those features of the model that are relevant for (1) a number of empirical observations and (2) the basic issue of temporally non-local states, expressed by violations of a temporal Bell inequality. We first summarize the ingredients of the quantum Zeno model and then transform the concepts to the NeckerâZeno model.

The quantum Zeno model describes a two-state system which is coupled to an environment. In the original model of Misra and Sudarshan (1977), the âenvironmentâ constitutes a measuring device and the corresponding process may be interpreted as an observation. More generally, however, this coupling describes a particular interaction with an environment such that the state of the system becomes stabilized. Note that âenvironmentâ can refer to any other degrees of freedom to which the two-state system is coupled and for which we do not take into account its dynamics.

Two complementary processes characterize the quantum Zeno model:
(D1)A continuous ârotationâ in a two-dimensional state space generated by a Hamiltonian urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0002, where
urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0003
and g is a coupling parameter determining the timescale of the rotation. The corresponding time evolution generated by this Hamiltonian describes the dynamics of the system for the case that the coupling to the environment is switched off. Essentially, it corresponds to a ârotationâ in the space of states, where the continuous transitions between the two possible states are realized by superpositions of the two possibilities.
(D2)A discontinuous âprojectionâ onto one of the two âeigenstatesâ urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0004 or urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0005 of the operator
urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0006
as the result of an âobservation.â
The eigenstates of urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0007 are stable under the âobservationâ (or, more generally, the coupling to the environment), but they are unstable under the dynamics according to urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0008. The corresponding two processes in (D1) and (D2) are complementary because the two matrices urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0009 and urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0010 do not commute:

(1)
Now we are interested in three different timescales:
The temporal duration urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0012 refers to the undisturbed evolution âwithout observationâ of the system (decoupled from the environment) and corresponds to the average time interval between two âswitchesâ of the decoupled system. This temporal scale is approximately given by urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0013.
The average dwell time ãTã is the interval between two switches of the system while it is âunder observation.â This requires a scale for the strength of the interaction of the system with the measuring device (or, more generally, to its environment).
The strength of this interaction can be expressed in terms of a third timescale ÎT. This timescale has the intuitive meaning of an âobservational updateâ interval, that is, successive observations of the state of the system are separated by ÎT.
One can derive (Atmanspacher et al., 2004) the following relation between these three timescales

2.2. Application to bistable perception
We now associate the formalism of the Zeno effect to bistable perception. First, we interpret the two dynamical processes of the Zeno model in terms of mental processes:
(D1')   If a subject does not observe the ambiguous stimulus, that is, if the stimulus is turned off, the preceding perceptual state âdecays.â The rapidity of this decay is related to the time constant urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0016 and is assumed to be of the order of 300 ms, in accordance with the electrophysiological P300 component in event-related potentials. (For more details of this interpretation, see Atmanspacher et al., 2004; see also Dehaene & Changeux, 2011.)
(D2')   Whenever a subject actually watches the stimulus, this is accompanied by a mental âupdateâ process. The mental state with its perspective of the cube is not stationary during the observation period, but it is updated after a time interval ÎT. This update interval can be associated with the timescale at which the sequence of successive stimuli can be mentally resolved, ÎTâ30 ms. (See again Atmanspacher et al., 2004 for further details, see also PÃ¶ppel, 1997.)3
With this mental interpretation of the Zeno effect in terms of the dynamics and timescales relevant for the perception of ambiguous stimuli, we can now interpret Eq. 2 with respect to bistable perception. Equation 2 then turns into a relation between the average dwell time ãTã of a perceived perspective (of the order of some seconds) and the timescales urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0017 and ÎT as introduced above.

Equation 2 is clearly satisfied for these three mental timescales. Moreover, the predictive power of the model can be convincingly demonstrated if it is possible to vary one of the timescales (urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0018) as an independent variable and measure another one (ãTã) as a function of urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0019. Assuming that ÎT remains constant, Eq. 2 predicts a quadratic dependence of ãTã on urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0020.

Such an experimental set-up is possible with discontinuous stimulus presentation. Under certain conditions, the timescale urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0021 can be approximated by the off-time in discontinuous presentation. As long as this off-time exceeds urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0022 ms, the corresponding scenario induces an âeffective Hamiltonianâ with altered period. This way, dwell times T (and their means ãTã) can be recorded as a function of off-time as an independent variable, which makes it possible to test Eq. 2 experimentally.

A comparison of observations by Kornmeier and Bach (2004); Kornmeier, Ehm, Bigalke, and Bach (2007); and Orbach, Zucker, and Olson (1966) with the predictions of the NeckerâZeno model is shown in Fig. 1. The plotted symbols show observed values of ãTã as a function of off-times. For off-times urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0023 ms, Fig. 1 clearly shows the dependence that the model predicts (Eq. 2). The best polynomial fit to the data (solid line) is quadratic and yields ÎTâ70 ms.

Note that off-times urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0024 ms in Fig. 1 clearly deviate from the curve predicted by Eq. 2. This is due to the fact that for off-times smaller than 300 ms, urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0025 can no longer be used to mimic urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0026, but it still has an influence on ãTã. For urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0027 ms, it is plausible to assume that ÎT becomes restrained by urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0028. In this situation, Eq. 2 predicts an increase in ãTã for decreasing urn:x-wiley:17568757:media:tops12044:tops12044-math-0029. (The details of the analysis have been described by Atmanspacher et al. 2008.)

This can be seen in data obtained by Kornmeier and Bach (2004), Kornmeier et al. (2007), and Orbach et al. (1966) for small off-times in Fig. 1. The NeckerâZeno model describes an increase in ãTã for decreasingly short off-times, reaching the empirically established dwell time of a few seconds for continuous presentation as off-time vanishes. This effect provides evidence for opposing trends for long and short off-times, separated by a critical timescale of the order of 300 ms.4 To our knowledge there is no other model that predicts this non-trivial behavior of dwell times correctly.

A proper understanding of holism and causation is the key to an appropriate interpretation of systems which violate Bell inequalities. This also applies to their temporal variant, although in a slightly different way. While the effects of non-locality (Ã  la Bell, 1966; Aspect et al., 1982) are mostly discussed in terms of spatial relations between spatial subsystems, a temporal Bell inequality refers to relations between temporal segments of the history of a system.

Violating a temporal Bell inequality entails that a state of a system is non-locally distributed along the time axis. System states are improperly described if they are sharply localized at definite instances of time. They are âstretchedâ over an extended time interval that may depend on the specific system considered. Within this interval, relations such as âearlierâ or âlaterâ are illegitimate designators of the system state. This is just another way of saying that it is impossible to define causal relationships within such a time interval (Filk & von MÃ¼ller, 2009).

In the NeckerâZeno model, this could be interpreted due to a âsuperpositionâ state, actualizing neither one nor the other perspective, but residing somehow âin between,â offering the potential to actualize either one or the other perspectival state. Needless to say, this resembles the idea of a âreductionâ in a quantum superposition state very closely. As mentioned in footnote 1, a similar scenario of mental temporal non-locality was recently proposed as characteristic of processes of cognitive decision making (Busemeyer et al., 2006).

It is tempting to relate this temporal non-locality to a âwindow of temporal nowness,â a concept that transcends a sharp boundary of presence between past and future (Filk & von MÃ¼ller, 2009; PÃ¶ppel, 1997). This idea dates back at least to James's notion of the âspecious present,â a present mental state extending over a time interval rather than fixed to an instant of vanishing duration. Acategorial states as discussed by Atmanspacher and Fach (2005) might be interesting candidates for temporal non-locality as a mental feature.

An interesting remark by Sudarshan (1983), one of the discoverers of the quantum Zeno effect, illustrates how the phenomenal experience of a temporally holistic state could be imagined. Sudarshan speculates about a mode of awareness in which âsensations, feelings, and insights are not neatly categorized into chains of thoughts, nor is there a step-by-step development of a logical-legal argument-to-conclusion. Instead, patterns appear, interweave, coexist; and sequencing is made inoperative. Conclusion, premises, feelings, and insights coexist in a manner defying temporal order.â

It is a necessary condition for temporally non-local correlations that the dynamics of the system considered is governed by operators that do not commute. This is the case in the NeckerâZeno model, so that bistable perception may indeed exhibit non-classical behavior although the corresponding cognitive mechanisms are not treated on the basis of a genuine quantum physical system. On a more general basis, we argued previously (Atmanspacher & Filk, 2003) that non-commuting time operators might be generic features of systems with temporal non-locality. It is known that such time operators bear an intimate connection with both inconsistent histories and temporal non-locality (Atmanspacher & Amann, 1998).

1 From a broader point of view, bistable perception can be considered as a low-level, spontaneous version of cognitive decision processes in general. For instance, Busemeyer, Wang, and Townsend (2006) presented effects of non-commutativity in cognitive decision making. More recently, Aerts, Gabora, and Sozzo (2013) and Bruza, Kitto, Ramm, and Sitbon (2013) proposed tests of Bell-type inequalities for decisions about conceptual combinations (in conceptual spaces).
2 This corresponds to the strategy that, to begin with, phenomena should primarily be discussed at the level of description at which they are observed. The rationale behind this is that their reduction to lower levels may be problematic if the correlations between levels are more sophisticated than one to one. Of course, it remains entirely legitimate and useful to study and establish such correlations.
3 Recent work by vanRullen, Reddy, and Koch (2005) suggests another empirical demonstration of ÎT of the same order of magnitude as an update interval: the âwagon wheelâ illusion. There is evidence for an attention dependence of ÎT in this scenario.
4 It was recently pointed out by Dehaene and Changeux (2011) that the timescale of â 300 ms is of crucial significance for the distinction between conscious and unconscious information processing.
5 Sometimes, in particular by proponents of the many-world interpretation of quantum mechanics (Everett, 1957; deWitt, 1970), objective realism is simply defined as the ontic existence of a quantum state. We interpret this ontic existence in the sense of âelements of realityâ as discussed by Einstein, Podolsky, and Rosen (1935). Caglioti\n2 A Quantum Representation\nA digression is in order for those who are less familiar with quantum mechanics. Being\na particle or a wave is not an intrinsic property for a quantum object like, e.g., photons,\nelectrons, atoms or even composite systems like molecules. Whether the objects of\nquantum mechanics behave like particles, with a kinetic energy E and a momentum p,\nor like waves, with an angular frequency x and a wavevector k, depends on the\nexperiments used for their investigation. Thus photons, the quanta of the electromagnetic ï¬eld currently considered as waves, behave like particles in the photoelectric\neffect, while neutrons, currently viewed as particles constituting with protons the\natomic nuclei, behave like waves when diffracted by a periodic crystal lattice structure.\nThe dual nature of quantum objects is expressed by the correspondence\nE Â¼ \u0001hx; p Â¼ \u0001hk, where \u0001h = 1.05 \u0003 10â34 J s is the reduced Planck constant, i.e. the\nPlanckâs constant h divided by 2p. Thus a stationary motion (eigenstate) of a quantum\nobject, either free or in a potential well and characterized by a given E, may also be\nviewed as a wave of angular frequency x. A basic concept in quantum mechanics is\nthat an eigenstate of a quantum object is not observable without perturbing it, i.e.,\nwithout producing a change of state; thus only transitions between eigenstates are\nobservable, and it is through the transitions between as many pairs of eigenstates as\npossible that we can know the energy of each eigenstate. The energy required to\nproduce a transition between two eigenstates of energy E+ and E- is given by the\ndifference E\u0004 \u0004 E Ã¾ Â¼ \u0001hÃ°x\u0004 \u0004 x Ã¾ Ã. Thus it is the angular frequency difference\nx\u0004 \u0004 x Ã¾ \u0005 x, corresponding to the beat of the frequencies associated with the two\neigenstates, that is observable.\nThe quantum behavior of the ammonia molecule NH3 is understood by an interesting property. Consider the two conï¬gurations in space of the molecule depicted in\nFig. 2a, where one is the specular image of the other. The spectroscopy of NH3 in the\ngaseous phase reveals a frequency of 24 GHz, which constituted the basis for the ï¬rst\nmicrowave ampliï¬ed stimulated emission of radiation (maser), invented by Charles H.\nTownes with J. Quantum dynamics allows for a\ncoherent oscillation of the three hydrogens and the N atom between the two conï¬guration by passing through the plane normal to the molecular axis while keeping the\ncenter of mass at rest. This apparently mysterious fact actually reveals a basic mechanism of quantum mechanics: the direct coherent tunneling of the three H atoms\nthrough the specular plane (Fig. 2b), and correspondingly of the N atom so as to keep\nthe center of mass ï¬xed [3], from the red to the blue conï¬guration and back.\nThe tunneling mechanism with a frequency in the microwave domain, besides\nbeing expected from quantum mechanical calculations, is conï¬rmed by its peculiar\nisotopic effect, i.e., by the change of frequency with replacement of H atoms with one\nor more D atoms [4]. Then\nthe process restarts. Abstract
We present a comprehensive concept for the fabric of reality and the creation of life through the generation and integration of information, modelled through toroidal processing of wave energy. We submit that human consciousness cannot be understood in a reductionist context, but that it is rather an expression of a cosmic modality or a universal consciousness. We therefore introduce a mental attribute of the entire cosmos that for our world, requires a wave-like holonomic description, implying non-material physicalism in a cosmopsychism context. It is postulated that the generation of life in the cosmos requires a symmetry breaking from a 4 th spatial dimension (5-D spacetime) involving a Sub-Quantum domain, that contains information conceptualized as a pro-active ontological essence. Thereby, a multi-layered fractal reality is conceived integrated by a universal toroidal/mÃ¶bius-ring type of connection. This is described at the level of a sub-Planckian scale, and is instrumental in past/future transactional information processing and pilot-wave guiding. In this framework, the Zero-point Energy Field is seen as a transition zone from the Sub-Quantum domain to our quantum world. In concert they provide an all-pervading superfluid quantum field that enables soliton (electron-phonon quasi-particle)-mediated interaction with non-neural brain compartments, in which hydro-ionic (hydronium ions and Ca2+-ions) are instrumental. In this process, freely moving protons form wave-antennas in the water matrices of the brain that can receive active life-information as the building blocks for conscious moments. Toroidal geometry is also involved in the functional organisation of wave-coherence and interference in brain, to assure a personal memory. In addition, we postulate a global memory workspace, associated with, but not reducible to the brain. This field-sensitive holographic workspace, that exhibits an event horizon information projection, is seen to be involved in predictive coding and quality control of awareness and can be conceived as personal double. The imprinting of life conditions in inanimate (pre-biotic) structures in biological evolution is conceived as a toroidal processing of energy-consciousness providing a non-dual recursive creation of reality. The unfolding of pre-mordial information into the future is described in geometric terms and defined in a "Beyond Fibonacci" type of spiral mathematics that also includes an extra 4 th spatial dimension. Recent studies of others indeed have shown that the creation of life can be conceived as a symmetry breaking of condensed bosons from a 5-D informational phase-space, supposedly through formation of magnetic monopoles in a Hicks setting, again using an essential toric information code. The monopoles, produced in this process, interact with DNA/RNA on the basis of the molecular entities of life such as H2O, carbon and nitrogen-bases, and this supports recent work on the role of oscillatory DNA wave resonance in cellular communication, being crucial for problem solving of life cells for survival in their environment. Boson-mediated symmetry breaking, modelled by toroidal spinor geometry was also described in a bifurcative self-interaction model that assumes supervenience of mental on physical states. It is finally concluded that scale invariant information, being expressed on the holographic event horizons of each individual cosmic entity, provides a unified connective principle that reveals an intrinsic mental aspect of the cosmos.

Study suggests the brain works like a resonance chamber
by Champalimaud Centre for the Unknown

Study suggests the brain works like a resonance chamber
Spectral power of fMRI signals differs significantly between anesthesia conditions. a Spatial maps of spectral power in 8 non-overlapping frequency bands, averaged across the sâ=â12 fMRI scans recorded under each anesthesia conditions and normalized by the mean power in sPMâ=â2 postmortem scans. fMRI recordings were recorded from nâ=â6 genetically similar female rats, each scanned twice in 3 anesthesia conditions (with isoflurane at 0%, 1%, and 3%. b Power in 8 frequency bands averaged across all brain voxels relative to the mean power in 2 postmortem scans. Dots correspond to the mean power in each scan and error bars represent the meanâÂ±âstandard error across the sâ=â12 scans. c Two-sided p-values obtained from 10,000 t-tests on randomly permuted data comparing the power in sâ=â12 scans over the 3 anesthesia conditions and in each of 8 non-overlapping frequency bands, reported with respect to the standard threshold of 0.05 and the Bonferroni-corrected threshold of 0.0021. Credit: Nature Communications (2023). DOI: 10.1038/s41467-023-36025-x

Researchers at the Champalimaud Foundation and the University of Minho, in Portugal, have found evidence of resonant waves in rat brain activity using ultrafast and ultrahigh field magnetic resonance imaging. Their work demonstrates from simple fundamental principles how such wavesâmuch in the manner of sound vibrations in a guitar chamberâcan form connections between distant brain areas, which are key for healthy brain function.


It's been over 20 years since neuroimaging studiesâusing functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI), a widely-used technology to capture live videos of brain activityâhave been detecting brain-wide complex patterns of correlated brain activity that appear disrupted in a wide range of neurological and psychiatric disorders. These patterns form spontaneously, even at rest when no particular task is being performed, and have been detected not only in humans but also across mammals, including monkeys and rodents.

Although such spatial patterns of correlated activation have been consistently detected across neuroimaging centers around the world, the nature of these correlations was not clear.

"We do not yet fully understand how the brain communicates over long distances. We know that distant areas exhibit signal correlations, and that they are implicated in brain function, but we do not completely understand their nature," says Noam Shemesh, principal investigator of the Preclinical MRI Lab at the Champalimaud Foundation, in Lisbon, and senior author of a study published in Nature Communications.

"In this study, we wanted to understand what lies underneath those correlations and investigate the mechanisms involved," stresses Shemesh.


The video shows that brain activity captured with fMRI can be reconstructed as the superposition of a small number of macroscopic stationary waves, or resonant modes, oscillating in time. Credit: Joana Cabral

A number of theoretical works had proposed that these patterns could be explained by standing waves (whose peaks and troughs do not move in space) resonating in the brain structureâthat is, by waves analogous to the modes of vibration in musical instruments. But there was little experimental evidence to support this hypothesis due to the poor temporal resolution of fMRI, reaching only an image or two per second.

"If we could find that the spatial patterns oscillate, this would provide evidence supporting the resonance hypothesis," says Joana Cabral, first author of the study, from the Life and Health Sciences Research Institute of the University of Minho and a visiting scientist in Shemesh's lab since 2019.

So what the team did was to speed up image acquisition, and they discovered that the signals in distant brain regions actually oscillate together in time. "These oscillatory patterns look like a higher-dimensional analog of resonance modes in musical instruments; they are akin to reverberations, to echoes inside the brain," says Cabral.

"Our data show that the complex spatial patterns are a result of transiently and independently oscillating underlying modes, just like individual instruments participate in creating a more complex piece in an orchestra," says Shemesh.

"The distinct modes, each contributing something to the overall picture at different time scales and different wavelengths, can be added up together, generating complex macroscopic patterns similar to the ones observed experimentally [see below]. To our knowledge, this is the first time that brain activity captured with fMRI is reconstructed as the superposition of standing waves," he points out.

The new study thus strongly points to a key role for these resonant waves, or modes, in brain function. These resonant phenomena, the authors believe, are at the root of the coherent, coordinated brain activity that is needed for normal brain function as a whole.


Ultrafast MRI
The researchers detected the resonant modes in rats in the resting state, which means the animals were not subjected to any specific external stimulus. Indeed, no tasks were needed, for as already mentioned, even when we (and mammals in general) are doing nothing in particular, our brains continue to generate spontaneous activity patterns that can be captured by fMRI.

To visualize the oscillations, the researchers created "videos" of activity using the potent ultrahigh-field experimental MRI scanner in Shemesh's lab and performed ultrafast experiments developed some time ago by that lab for other purposes.

"Noam and I met in 2019, and we decided to obtain recordings of brain activity at the maximum temporal resolution we could achieve in the 9.4 Tesla scanner at his lab," recalls Cabral. "Noam designed the experiments, Francisca Fernandes [the third author of the study] performed them, and I did the data analysis and the visualization. Noam managed to achieve a temporal resolution of 26 images per second, and thus obtained 16,000 images per 10 minute scan (instead of 600 images at the typical resolution of one image per second)."

Like waves in the ocean
"When we first saw the videos of the recorded brain activity, we saw clear waves of activity, like waves in the ocean, propagating in complex patterns within the cortex and the striatum [a subcortical region of the forebrain]", says Cabral.

"And we found that the signals could be described by the superposition of a small number of macroscopic stationary waves, or resonant modes, oscillating in time. Notably, each standing wave was found to cover extended areas of the brain, with peaks distributed in distinct cortical and subcortical structures, forming functional networks."

The researchers experimented with rats in three different conditions: sedated, lightly anesthetised and deeply anesthetised. (In fact, the animals were lightly sedated in the resting state, to avoid any discomfort to them.) "The spatial configuration of these stationary waves was very consistent across rats scanned in the same condition," Cabral points out.

Shemesh adds, "We showed that brain functional networks are driven by resonance phenomena. This explains the correlations that are otherwise observed when you do slow imaging. Long-range brain interactions are governed by a 'flow' of information that is oscillatory and repetitive."

Pathological states
They also found that increasing the amount of anesthetic reduces the number, frequency and duration of the resonant stationary waves. As already mentioned, previous studies have shown that certain patterns of brain activation are consistently altered in disorders of consciousness. So this experimental design, says Cabral, was actually also meant to mimic different pathological states.ChatGPTChatGPTThe study you mentioned, conducted by researchers at the Champalimaud Foundation and the University of Minho, provides fascinating insights into brain function and its potential resonant nature. Here's a summary of the key points:Resonant Waves in Brain Activity: The study found evidence of resonant waves in rat brain activity using ultrafast and ultrahigh field magnetic resonance imaging (MRI). These waves are akin to sound vibrations in a guitar chamber and form connections between distant brain areas, crucial for healthy brain function.Spatial Patterns of Brain Activity: For over 20 years, neuroimaging studies have detected complex patterns of correlated brain activity in various neurological and psychiatric disorders. The hypothesis was that these patterns could be explained by standing waves resonating in the brain, similar to vibration modes in musical instruments.Ultrafast MRI Imaging: By speeding up image acquisition, the researchers discovered that signals in distant brain regions oscillate together in time. Conclusion
The invention of soliton transmission, predicted in 1973 and experimentally demonstrated in 1980, is one of the major discoveries made in the area of optical telecommunication. Although from the period of invention of optical solitons up to very recent years the main line of research activities was based on the concept of the âclassical solitonâ, which represents an exact balance between the fiber group-velocity dispersion and its intensity-dependent refractive index, most recent experimental and theoretical developments focus on dispersion-managed solitons. It is now widely recognized that DM solitons in fiber-optic links are the most promising way both to upgrade the capacity of existing terrestrial telecommunication networks and to design submarine fiber systems. Most of the current research and development activities in this context aim to develop a non-empirical methodology of designing ultrahigh-speed DM fiber systems.ChatGPTChatGPTThe conclusion from the article on "Optical Fiber Solitons" highlights the significant advancements and potential of soliton transmission in optical telecommunications. Here's a breakdown of the key points:Historical Milestones: The concept of soliton transmission in optical fibers was predicted in 1973 and experimentally demonstrated in 1980. This marks a major milestone in the field of optical telecommunication.Classical Solitons: Initially, research focused on 'classical solitons', which are a balance between fiber group-velocity dispersion and the intensity-dependent refractive index of the fiber. This balance is crucial for maintaining the shape and speed of the soliton over long distances.Dispersion-Managed (DM) Solitons: More recent developments have shifted towards dispersion-managed solitons. DM solitons are seen as a more effective approach for managing the dispersion properties of optical fibers, which is essential for maintaining the integrity of the soliton over long transmission distances.Applications in Telecommunication Networks: DM solitons are considered the most promising technology for upgrading the capacity of existing terrestrial telecommunication networks and for designing new submarine fiber systems. This indicates a significant potential for improving global communication infrastructure.Current Research and Development: The focus of current research is on developing a non-empirical methodology for designing ultrahigh-speed DM fiber systems. This involves a systematic approach to optimize the performance and reliability of these systems.Overall, the development and application of solitons in optical fiber technology represent a crucial advancement in telecommunications, offering the potential for faster, more reliable, and higher-capacity data transmission across global networks.YouI decided to call the conversion Soliton Chambers

Spin-resolution might refer to the ability of the brain to distinguish or utilize these different spin states, potentially in the context of quantum information processing.Integrating these concepts suggests a model where brain function involves complex interactions between wave-like neural activities, fluid dynamics, electromagnetic fields, and possibly even quantum mechanical effects. This model is highly speculative and would require substantial evidence to support it, especially regarding the quantum aspects. It aligns with some of the more exploratory theories in neuroscience and quantum biology but remains outside the mainstream scientific understanding of brain function.YouI was a little unclear, by synchronizing electromagnetic gate induction I was referencing this theory: Joural of Integrative Neuroscience, Vol. 14, No. 3 (2015) 431-435

The reference to "relativistic" in the title has nothing to do with Einstein's theory of
relativity, but rather it refers to a brain that is relative to one's own point of view, i.e..
he brain's own internal account of material reality as opposed to a software/hard
ware dichotomy implied in computational approaches of brain function. In the
Relativistic Brain, the authors have set a course on explaining why higher brain
function cannot be simulated by a Turing machine. Indeed what they argue is for
computationalism" to be replaced as the field ignores essential issues for under-
standing how the brain works. These issues relate to physiological, mathematical,
evolutionary, and information arguments that explain away computational neuro-
science as a field not much different from artificial intelligence
The Relativistic Brain offers a complete paradigm shift on how we comprehend the
brain to work. Electrical firing of digital spikes propagating along axons in widely
listributed networks of axons form bundles of white matter that continuously gen
erate neuronal electromagnetic fields (NEMFs). The continuous interferences of these
NEMFs defines the neuronal space-time continuum form which "mental-space"
emerges that underlies higher-order brain functions, including consciousness. How
(does this neuronal space-time continuum emerge? The superposition of time-varying
NEMFs form bulkier white matter from "biological coils" that enable strong NEMFs
to "glue" the digital component (neurons) of the brain together. The combination of
NEMFs provides the "physiological glue" for the creation of the neuronal space-time
continuum defined as the "mental-space" over widespread areas of the brain that
include both cortical and subcortical structures.

Nicolelis, as reviewed in the Journal of Integrative Neuroscience, proposes a novel perspective on brain function, particularly in relation to consciousness and higher-order brain functions. Let's break down the key points:Neuronal Electromagnetic Fields (NEMFs): The theory suggests that the brain's function is heavily influenced by electromagnetic fields generated by neuronal activity. These fields are not just byproducts of neural firing but play an active role in shaping brain function.Neuronal Space-Time Continuum: The superposition of these time-varying NEMFs creates what the authors refer to as a "neuronal space-time continuum." This concept seems to be a metaphorical way of describing how the combined effects of these fields influence brain activity across both space (different brain regions) and time.Mental-Space Emergence: From this neuronal space-time continuum, a "mental-space" emerges, which underlies higher-order brain functions, including consciousness. This mental-space is thought to be a product of the complex interactions of NEMFs across widespread areas of the brain.Physiological Glue: The authors describe the NEMFs as providing a "physiological glue" that integrates the digital component (neurons) of the brain. This suggests a model where electromagnetic interactions are fundamental to the cohesion and integration of brain processes.Critique of Computationalism: The book argues against the reduction of brain function to computational algorithms, suggesting that such approaches overlook the complex, dynamic, and integrative nature of brain activity as influenced by NEMFs.Biological Coils and White Matter: The concept of "biological coils" generating strong NEMFs that contribute to the formation of white matter is an interesting one. It implies a more active role for white matter in brain function, beyond just facilitating neural connections.This theory presents a significant shift from traditional computational models of brain function, emphasizing the role of electromagnetic fields and their complex interactions in shaping consciousness and higher-order brain functions. It's a holistic approach that integrates physiological, mathematical, and evolutionary perspectives to offer a more comprehensive understanding of how the brain works. However, it's important to note that while intriguing, these ideas are still speculative and would require further empirical validation to be widely accepted in the scientific community.YouOk the point is that computation can occur across a lot of scales, simultaneously, using different substrates, in the same individual: Computer Science > Multiagent Systems

There's Plenty of Room Right Here: Biological Systems as Evolved, Overloaded, Multi-scale Machines
Joshua Bongard, Michael Levin
Download PDF
The applicability of computational models to the biological world is an active topic of debate. We argue that a useful path forward results from abandoning hard boundaries between categories and adopting an observer-dependent, pragmatic view. Such a view dissolves the contingent dichotomies driven by human cognitive biases (e.g., tendency to oversimplify) and prior technological limitations in favor of a more continuous, gradualist view necessitated by the study of evolution, developmental biology, and intelligent machines. Efforts to re-shape living systems for biomedical or bioengineering purposes require prediction and control of their function at multiple scales. This is challenging for many reasons, one of which is that living systems perform multiple functions in the same place at the same time. We refer to this as "polycomputing" - the ability of the same substrate to simultaneously compute different things. This ability is an important way in which living things are a kind of computer, but not the familiar, linear, deterministic kind; rather, living things are computers in the broad sense of computational materials as reported in the rapidly-growing physical computing literature. We argue that an observer-centered framework for the computations performed by evolved and designed systems will improve the understanding of meso-scale events, as it has already done at quantum and relativistic scales. Here, we review examples of biological and technological polycomputing, and develop the idea that overloading of different functions on the same hardware is an important design principle that helps understand and build both evolved and designed systems. Learning to hack existing polycomputing substrates, as well as evolve and design new ones, will have massive impacts on regenerative medicine, robotics, and computer engineeringChatGPTChatGPTThe concept of "polycomputing" as described in the paper by Joshua Bongard and Michael Levin is indeed a fascinating and innovative approach to understanding biological systems. Here's a breakdown of the key points:Observer-Dependent, Pragmatic View: The authors advocate for a perspective that moves away from rigid categorizations and embraces a more fluid understanding of biological systems. This approach recognizes the influence of human cognitive biases and technological limitations on our understanding of biology.Polycomputing: This term refers to the ability of a single substrate (like a biological system) to perform multiple computational processes simultaneously. It challenges the traditional view of computers as linear, deterministic machines, suggesting instead that living systems are more akin to "computational materials" that can process information in diverse, overlapping ways.Multi-Scale Functionality: Living systems are highlighted for their ability to perform different functions at the same time within the same space, across multiple scales. This complexity makes prediction and control for biomedical or bioengineering purposes challenging.Evolution and Design: The paper discusses how understanding polycomputing can improve our grasp of both evolved (natural) and designed (artificial) systems. This understanding can be crucial for fields like regenerative medicine, robotics, and computer engineering.Overloading Functions on Hardware: The concept that different functions can be overloaded on the same biological "hardware" is seen as a key design principle in both natural and artificial systems. This principle can help in understanding complex biological phenomena and in designing more efficient and versatile technological systems.Implications for Various Fields: Learning to manipulate existing polycomputing systems and to develop new ones could have significant impacts on various fields, particularly in creating more advanced and efficient technologies that mimic the complexity of biological systems.In summary, the paper proposes a paradigm shift in how we view and understand biological systems, emphasizing their computational complexity and versatility. This perspective not only enhances our understanding of biology but also opens up new avenues for technological innovation by drawing inspiration from the intricate computing capabilities of living organisms.YouA list of all of the topics we've discussed.The previous model used in this conversation is unavailable.
